<div id="review_2073600247" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2073600247" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2073600247?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Sara by: </span>
      Southern Literary Trail
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=kindle-purchase">kindle-purchase</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=southern-lit">southern-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2073600247" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12702555621900342664">Before I can tell you how much I loved this book, I must tell you that my father, like Jayber Crow, was a barber in a time when the barber shop was a social place and not a styling salon. I loved this very male place, where I could very rarely slip myself into a corner and listen to old men talk about the weather and the crops and gossip about one another in a friendly and civil way. My father was also a self-taught fiddler and music would often pour from the back room of the shop well into the </span>
  <span id="freeText12702555621900342664" style="display:none">Before I can tell you how much I loved this book, I must tell you that my father, like Jayber Crow, was a barber in a time when the barber shop was a social place and not a styling salon. I loved this very male place, where I could very rarely slip myself into a corner and listen to old men talk about the weather and the crops and gossip about one another in a friendly and civil way. My father was also a self-taught fiddler and music would often pour from the back room of the shop well into the night. I thought of this place as a crossroads of our community. There was no one that my father did not know and know well.<br /><br />With this in mind, I was thrilled to meet Jayber Crow, a bachelor barber in Port William, Kentucky, who serves his community with so much more than a haircut and a shave. Wendell Berry opens this man’s life and heart to us and through his interactions with the community in Port William, the lives of his neighbors and friends. The story unfolds, with vignettes sprinkled throughout detailing the events that make up the everyday lives of the entire community. But, most importantly, we meet Jayber, whose boyhood is laced with both tragedy and rescue, and whose bachelorhood is really as peopled with love and attachment as a life can be.<br /><br />Wendell Berry has a marvelously descriptive and captivating style. It sometimes felt to me as if I were sitting in that barber shop and listening in on the conversation, or on the bank of the river pole in hand, the story unfolded in such a charming and folksy manner. But this book is anything but surface material, there is depth galore and major issues tackled in profound ways.<br /><br /><i>	Prayer is like lying awake at night, afraid, with your head under the cover, hearing only the beating of your own heart. It is like a bird that has blundered down the flue and is caught indoors and flutters at the windowpanes. It is like standing a long time on a cold day, knocking at a shut door.<br />	But sometimes a prayer comes that you have not thought to pray, yet suddenly there it is and you pray it. Sometimes you just trustfully and easily pass into the other world of sleep. Sometimes the bird finds that what looks like an opening is an opening, and it flies away. Sometimes the shut door opens and you go through it into the same world you were in before, in which you belong as you did not before.</i><br /><br />I could quote dozens of passages that address such issues and do them with a candor and feeling that is seldom encountered. I can see myself re-reading this book someday and finding hidden nuggets of truth and wisdom buried deep beneath the story line that were missed the first time around. <br /><br />As is often so, I owe a debt of gratitude to <i>
  <b>The Southern Literary Trail </b>
</i>group for introducing me to this remarkable author. I know this will not be the last book I read by Mr. Berry. I wish I had discovered him earlier...but, as Jayber would agree, I am sure, it is better to meet a good man late than to never meet him at all.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12702555621900342664" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_610138508" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/610138508" />
    <a title="Connie G" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5364077-connie-g"><img alt="Connie G" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307502493p2/5364077.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/610138508?book_show_action=true">Aug 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Connie G" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Connie G" href="/user/show/5364077-connie-g">Connie G</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=kentucky">kentucky</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer610138508" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13792378788989513251">It was such a pleasure to read Wendell Berry's lyrical prose about a quiet, observant man living through the 20th Century. Jayber Crow was orphaned twice as a young boy, first when his parents died and then when his adoptive aunt and uncle passed away. He was sent to an orphanage, spent time at a divinity school until he questioned his faith, and worked some small jobs. But the river that flowed near his rural childhood home called to him, and he made his way back to Port William, Kentucky.<br/><br/>Jaybe</span>
  <span id="freeText13792378788989513251" style="display:none">It was such a pleasure to read Wendell Berry's lyrical prose about a quiet, observant man living through the 20th Century. Jayber Crow was orphaned twice as a young boy, first when his parents died and then when his adoptive aunt and uncle passed away. He was sent to an orphanage, spent time at a divinity school until he questioned his faith, and worked some small jobs. But the river that flowed near his rural childhood home called to him, and he made his way back to Port William, Kentucky.<br /><br />Jayber became the barber in Port William, and later added a few part-time jobs as a grave digger and church janitor. The barbershop was a wonderful atmosphere to introduce the residents of Port William to Jayber. "It's a fact that knowledge comes to barbers, just as stray cats come to milking barns. If you are a barber and you stay in one place long enough, eventually you will know the outlines of a lot of stories, and you will see how the bits and pieces of knowledge fit in." (94)<br /><br />He became part of the small Kentucky town, and had a vision of a gathered community. "What I saw now was the community imperfect and irresolute but held together by the frayed and always fraying, incomplete and yet ever-holding bonds of the various sorts of affection. There had maybe never been anybody who had not been loved by somebody, who had been loved by somebody else, and so on and on." (205)<br /><br />As the 20th Century marched on, Jayber chronicled wars, births and deaths, strong friendships, and a secret unrequited love for a married woman. He saw traditional farming methods being replaced by larger agribusinesses that took on substantial loans. Jayber saw Port William change as the interstate cut through farms. "It divided neighbor from neighbor. It made distant what had been close, and close what had been distant." (281)<br /><br />As an older man, Jayber moved to a small home by the river. Wendell Berry, who is also a poet, shows us Jayber's wisdom, his reverence for nature, the change of seasons, and his desire for self-sufficiency. I hated to turn the last page and see this beautiful book come to an end.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13792378788989513251" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_603242805" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/603242805" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4086118-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567611029p2/4086118.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/603242805?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/4086118-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Michael by: </span>
      Steve aka Sckenda
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=rural-life">rural-life</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=kentucky">kentucky</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=social-commentary">social-commentary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=farming">farming</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer603242805" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17703780502303640744">A moving and uplifting portrait of heaven. That’s how Jayber Crow sees it as he reflects on his life as an ordinary man living his life as a barber in close connection to his community in rural Kentucky, Port William. Or maybe I should say an extraordinary man in an ordinary community. What makes Crow special is that he believes in love, even to the point of trying his best to love his enemies. On my part, I found it easy to love him and hated for my time harvesting his wisdom and sensibilities </span>
  <span id="freeText17703780502303640744" style="display:none">A moving and uplifting portrait of heaven. That’s how Jayber Crow sees it as he reflects on his life as an ordinary man living his life as a barber in close connection to his community in rural Kentucky, Port William. Or maybe I should say an extraordinary man in an ordinary community. What makes Crow special is that he believes in love, even to the point of trying his best to love his enemies. On my part, I found it easy to love him and hated for my time harvesting his wisdom and sensibilities to end. To me he is an unforgettable character and the book a timeless vision of a rural way of life I cherish from my own upbringing.<br /><br />Like Berry himself, Crow had some early time with a farming community in north central Kentucky that set a baseline of Eden in his heart, which drew his return after some wanderings in the world. Crow is orphaned at a young age and is raised until the age of 11 by an aunt and uncle. But they die, and he ends up in a distant orphanage. From there he has a stint on scholarship at a divinity school. But he eventually realizes he really doesn’t believe in prayer: “if Jesus’ prayer in the garden wasn’t granted, what is there for us to pray, except ‘thy will be done’, which there is no use in praying because it will be done anyhow?” After an aimless period of menial jobs, he finds himself at one point magnetically pulled back to his true home, Port Royal, a young man in his early twenties in 1937.<br /><br />He feels the sense of home immediately in the acceptance he receives and in the help given him to set up a barber shop. Most of the men pass through his establishment, where all their hopes and concerns and all the news and rumors are shared. He sees his barber shop as effectively the living room of the town. it’s a fact that knowledge comes to barbers, just as stray cats come to milking barns. He experiences the company of women when they bring their children it, as well as through his extra work as a janitor at a Protestant church. He experiences the gravity of life’s travails through his other work as a gravedigger. Otherwise not much happens. He partakes of friendships from all walks of life, thrives on the seasons of gardening, fishing, and hiking the woods or along the river, and experiences the pervasive impacts on the community of the Depression, World War 2, and the boom times afterward. I appreciated the rhythm of his days when he retires to live in a cabin on the Kentucky River. An unrequited love for a married woman, Mattie, dwells sad and wonderful in his mind throughout much of the book.<br /><br />It is in his reflections on the life he observes that makes his relatively uneventful journey a compelling quest of the spirit and lesson of hope for sustaining values threatened by the inevitable changes wrought by modernism. I have to forgive him for trying to tell his conclusions rather that showing them through the narrative. Having just read some of his essays on the destructiveness of modern agribusiness and forestry on sustainable local economies, I see how many of the characters in this book serve as means to illustrate his ideas. It does bring the issues to life in personal terms, but the spelling out of Berry’s philosophy through Crow breaks some of my engagement in the story. <br /><br />Here are some illustrations of Crow’s attractive blend of pragmatic and spiritual outlook on life.<br /><br />On one’s possession by love:<br /><i>So it is that the life force may take possession of a man—so that in the end he may be possessed by something greater, no longer at all belonging to himself.</i><br /><br />On grief and its tapping of eternity:<br /><i>The grief had something in it of generosity, some nearness to joy. In a strange way it added to me what I had lost. I saw that, for me, the country would always be populated with presences and absences, presences of absences, the living and the dead. The world as it is would always be a reminder of the world that was, and of the world as it is to come. </i><br /><br />His critique of agribusiness in a nutshell:<br /><i>The new way of farming was a way of dependence, not on land and creatures and nature but on machines and fuel and chemicals of all sorts, bought things, and on the sellers of bought things—which finally made it a dependence on credit. </i><br /><br />A distillation of his view of community as imperfect, transitory, but somehow timeless:<br /><i>It was a community always disappointed in itself, disappointing its members, always trying to contain its divisions and gentle its meanness, always failing and yet always preserving a sort of will toward goodwill. I knew that, in the midst of all the ignorance and error, this was a membership; it was the membership of Port William and no other place on earth. My vision gathered the community as it never has been and never will be gathered in this world of time, for the community must always be marred by members who are indifferent to it or against it. And yet I saw them all as somehow perfected, beyond time, by one another’s love, compassion, and forgiveness, as it is said we may be perfected by grace.<br /><br />And so there we all were on a little wave of time lifting up to eternity, and none of us ever in time would know what to make of it. How could we? It is a mystery, for we are eternal beings living in time. Did I ever think that anybody would understand it? Yes. Once. I thought once that I would finally understand it.</i><br /><br />I am grateful for being turned to the book by Steve's wonderful review: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/350380434" rel="nofollow noopener">Review of Jayber Crow</a>. It's great I can look forward to other books by Berry about this fictional but real community of Port Royal.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17703780502303640744" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_41748489" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41748489" />
    <a title="Kj" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/298072-kj"><img alt="Kj" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1490607539p2/298072.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41748489?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kj" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kj" href="/user/show/298072-kj">Kj</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/298072-kj?shelf=own-it">own-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/298072-kj?shelf=seattle-school-of-theology">seattle-school-of-theology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/298072-kj?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41748489" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3538843593307214979">An easy five star rating for this one- half way through I already knew it had become one of my favorite books.<br/><br/>Not driven by plot, in fact not "driven" in any sense, this is a story that walks you gently and honestly through many parts of a life. It's hard to put words to a reading experience such as this one that takes you deep into the heart of what it means to live, to be alive. Despite its fiction narrative, "Jayber Crow" feels far closer to a guided mediation on youth and age, Progress vs. n</span>
  <span id="freeText3538843593307214979" style="display:none">An easy five star rating for this one- half way through I already knew it had become one of my favorite books.<br /><br />Not driven by plot, in fact not "driven" in any sense, this is a story that walks you gently and honestly through many parts of a life. It's hard to put words to a reading experience such as this one that takes you deep into the heart of what it means to live, to be alive. Despite its fiction narrative, "Jayber Crow" feels far closer to a guided mediation on youth and age, Progress vs. nature, community vs. materialism. About every five pages, a passage would knock me over with its distillation of truth and beauty from that which is most obvious and taken for granted. Over and over, I read things that made me say "I have always thought/felt that, and no one has ever put language to it." I've never used "integrity" to describe a book, but that is what this book showed me, and in a way that feels livable and desirable. <br /><br />Wendell Berry's heart for the things that run deeper than the passing of years, leads me, more than anything I have yet to encounter, towards an understanding of where our American culture sold its soul in exchange for borrowed fantasies of what qualifies as a life. And at the same time, this is one of the most hope-giving narratives I've ever had the blessing to sit with night after night.<br /><br />Oh read it. Read it. I don't know what else to say.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3538843593307214979" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2243275090" />
    <a title="Antoinette" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5328952-antoinette"><img alt="Antoinette" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503842818p2/5328952.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2243275090?book_show_action=true">Nov 02, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Antoinette" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Antoinette" href="/user/show/5328952-antoinette">Antoinette</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2243275090" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1756482357123470839">3.5 STARS.<br/><br/>“ I will stand like a tree and be in myself as I am.”<br/><br/>This book as the title indicates is about Jayber Crow. He is looking back on his life and reflecting on the paths he took, to get back to where home was for him. <br/><br/>Jayber Crow is a good, kind man- I would hazard to say, too good. He has lived in Port Williams for most of his life, but for the most part, he has always felt like a bystander.<br/><br/>I would call this a philosophical book. Jayber ponders religion, his place in the world, the justi</span>
  <span id="freeText1756482357123470839" style="display:none">3.5 STARS.<br /><br />“ I will stand like a tree and be in myself as I am.”<br /><br />This book as the title indicates is about Jayber Crow. He is looking back on his life and reflecting on the paths he took, to get back to where home was for him. <br /><br />Jayber Crow is a good, kind man- I would hazard to say, too good. He has lived in Port Williams for most of his life, but for the most part, he has always felt like a bystander.<br /><br />I would call this a philosophical book. Jayber ponders religion, his place in the world, the justification for war, nature, people and all the changes that come in this twentieth century.<br /><br />Wendell Berry is a lyrical writer. His words flow much like a stream. There is a gentleness to his prose.<br /><br />A few lines I’d like to share:<br /><br />“I’d had the idea once, that if I could get the chance before I died I would read all the good books there were.....I could see that if I got them all read and had no more surprises in that line, I would have been sorry.”<br /><br />“ The war years were long because we were always waiting for what we feared and for what we hoped. We felt, I think, that we were failing one another, for we needed to have something we could do for one another and mostly there was nothing. And yet, even in failing one another, even in our silence, we kept with one another.”<br /><br />What put me off this book was the overabundance of philosophizing. I love thoughtful books which this is, but when it starts to feel like a lecture, my brain turns off.<br /><br />I recently read Ebenezer LePage, which had many similarities to this book. I maybe should have held off reading this one to appreciate it more.<br /><br />There many many 5 Star reviews for this book. Could just have been the wrong time for this book for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1756482357123470839" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3667806689977588409">Pet peeves:<br/><br/>1.) Cars with dealer license plate frames. You bought the car, is it necessary to advertise where you bought it from? For free? This is America, you dumbass. Have some self-respect.<br/><br/>2.) Company vehicles that have an overhead dome light that has some sort of short in it that causes it to light-up whenever I go over railroad tracks, potholes, or spare change in the road at a speed of greater than 3 MPH and consequently makes me feel like the centerpiece in some hackneyed corporate motiva</span>
  <span id="freeText3667806689977588409" style="display:none">Pet peeves:<br /><br />1.) Cars with dealer license plate frames. You bought the car, is it necessary to advertise where you bought it from? For free? This is America, you dumbass. Have some self-respect.<br /><br />2.) Company vehicles that have an overhead dome light that has some sort of short in it that causes it to light-up whenever I go over railroad tracks, potholes, or spare change in the road at a speed of greater than 3 MPH and consequently makes me feel like the centerpiece in some hackneyed corporate motivational workshop complete with strobe lights.<br /><br />3.) Novels written in the first-person that supposedly take place in the American South during the Great Depression that present a narrator that never made it past the eighth grade but somehow manages to sound like Truman Capote and John Waters had a baby and left it in the care of Mr. and Mrs. Thurston Howell. As cool as it may sound, it doesn't work.<br /><br />This novel is unconvincing. As unconvincing as the bucolic America it conjures. It's sentimental pap and I couldn’t finish it. Couldn't really even begin it.<br /><br />Bucolic America is a commodity. It's a shiny bauble that you don't need but which has been foisted upon you by a culture that thinks better of itself than it deserves. <br /><br />Here's the good news: that culture is scrapping the bottom of the barrel if it's promoting the <a href="http://www.kval.com/news/offbeat/120317439.html?ref=guiltypleasures" rel="nofollow noopener">particular sentimentality</a> this rustic boob is best acquainted with. <br /><br />Lately, I've been inclined to think of Goodreads as some sort of psychological experiment; that all the personalities I interact with on here are really fronts for one or two graduate students somewhere. Graduate students who alternately fuck with and flatter me in some morbidly-algorithmic-impregnable manner such that I jump through my assigned hoops just so. This is a ridiculous notion, of course. But it is an easy explanation. And people, of which I am one, like easy explanations. <br /><br />America is an easy explanation.<br /><br />As you may have deduced, I drive a lot. For work, that easiest of explanations. I drive through and around a part of the country that many would absent-mindedly, if assuredly, consider bucolic. Once, late at night, I was driving down a quiet byway and nearly ran over a young man. He was sitting, cross-legged, square in the middle of the road. He looked placidly anesthetized. I missed hitting him by mere inches. His expression never changed as I, heart racing, palms sweaty, mouth cursing, eyes frozen wide, swerved around him in a mass of metal propelled at a speed that would have obliterated the human body. <br /><br />I didn't stop. </span>

<div id="review_1451988621" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1451988621" />
    <a title="``Laurie Henderson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18430919-laurie-henderson"><img alt="``Laurie Henderson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1394490737p2/18430919.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1451988621?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="``Laurie Henderson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="``Laurie Henderson" href="/user/show/18430919-laurie-henderson">``Laurie Henderson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18430919-laurie-henderson?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18430919-laurie-henderson?shelf=southern-setting">southern-setting</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18430919-laurie-henderson?shelf=appalachia">appalachia</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1451988621" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5445340509269763088">In the beginning Jayber Crow was a happy child living with his parents in northern Kentucky where life was still pretty primitive. No electricity or indoor plumbing and they grew all their own food. Jayber's parents had died of the flu after WW1 so his childless Aunt and Uncle brought him home to live giving him everything a child could possible need.<br/><br/>The lyrical descriptions of the countryside and Jayber's childhood reminded me of the book <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/159582.The_Yearling' title='The Yearling by Marjorie Kinnan Rawlings' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Yearling</a> where young Jody ran wild in rural Florida </span>
  <span id="freeText5445340509269763088" style="display:none">In the beginning Jayber Crow was a happy child living with his parents in northern Kentucky where life was still pretty primitive. No electricity or indoor plumbing and they grew all their own food. Jayber's parents had died of the flu after WW1 so his childless Aunt and Uncle brought him home to live giving him everything a child could possible need.<br /><br />The lyrical descriptions of the countryside and Jayber's childhood reminded me of the book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/159582.The_Yearling" title="The Yearling by Marjorie Kinnan Rawlings" rel="nofollow noopener">The Yearling</a> where young Jody ran wild in rural Florida with his pet deer, Flag. Both Jayber and Jody are well loved and happy children but both children soon had a brutal ending to their idyllic and magical childhood.<br /><br />Spoilers Below Beware!<br /><br />Jayber Crow's elderly parents would both die while he was still a child and he was taken to an orphanage to live his teenage years.<br /><br />Young Jody's magical childhood ends when his father is permanently crippled and preteen Jody had to take on all the responsibilities that his father was no longer able to fulfill.<br /><br />The Yearling thankfully ended at that point with the reader now aware that Jody would be poor and miserable for the rest of his life. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/46158.Marjorie_Kinnan_Rawlings" title="Marjorie Kinnan Rawlings" rel="nofollow noopener">Marjorie Kinnan Rawlings</a> would not write another 300 pages going into cruel detail of Jody's difficult new life. <br /><br />The author of Jayber Crow, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8567.Wendell_Berry" title="Wendell Berry" rel="nofollow noopener">Wendell Berry</a>, did decide to write another 300 pages detailing how Jayber's soul was crushed in the orphanage. Jayber returns to his original home town as the town barber where he still had a few childhood friends. But Jayber will live his life on the sidelines, listening to the talk in the barber shop of events in his customer's lives while his life remains unfulfilled.<br /><br />Maybe I just didn't have the stomach for this tale of unrelenting woe as related by the rather garrulous Jayber Crow. The author did bring the beautiful, rural Kentucky alive for this reader and I wouldn't mind taking a vacation to Kentucky now to see it for myself.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5445340509269763088" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText9091741507962086283" style="display:none"><b>5★</b><br />Less is more.<br />If the terms environmental activist, organic farming, or sustainable development have a place in your personal beliefs or you’re a fan of Thoreau, Muir, or Gandhi, you will no doubt appreciate or love Wendell Berry and this book. <div id="review_3616236156" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3616236156" />
    <a title="JimZ" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/99765491-jimz"><img alt="JimZ" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1575257175p2/99765491.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3616236156?book_show_action=true">Oct 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="JimZ" class="user" itemprop="url" name="JimZ" href="/user/show/99765491-jimz">JimZ</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3616236156" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7680490947762452523">This is one of a number of books and short stories Wendell Berry has written on the fiction town of Port William in Kentucky. There are characters from this novel that appear in other works of his. I read “Hannah Coulter” and liked it a great deal. She was married to Nathan Coulter, and he is mentioned in this book (I think he is mentioned....never mind that I just read it!)..<br/><br/>There is a passage from “Hannah Coulter” as I was reading it that I very much appreciated, and I am glad I placed it in m</span>
  <span id="freeText7680490947762452523" style="display:none">This is one of a number of books and short stories Wendell Berry has written on the fiction town of Port William in Kentucky. There are characters from this novel that appear in other works of his. I read “Hannah Coulter” and liked it a great deal. She was married to Nathan Coulter, and he is mentioned in this book (I think he is mentioned....never mind that I just read it!)..<br /><br />There is a passage from “Hannah Coulter” as I was reading it that I very much appreciated, and I am glad I placed it in my review of that book, because it was reminiscent of one of the passages that particularly stuck with me from “Jayber Crow”, and it has to do with the lives we lead and have led and will lead — that we must not fret about what might have been, must not fret about “what if” we had lived the life of somebody else we know, had we lived in a different time, had we made a different decision than the one we had made (that’s difficult...for me, and I imagine for lots of people). I’ll first cite the passage from “Jayber Crow”, and then from “Hannah Coulter”:<br />•	“…Theoretically, there is always a better place for a person to live, better work to do, a better spouse to wed, better friends to have. But then this person must meet herself coming back: Theoretically, there always is a better inhabitant of this place, a better member of this community, a better, worker, spouse, and friend than she is. This surely describes one of the circles of Hell, and who hasn’t travelled around it a time or two?...I have got to the age now where I can see how short a time we have to be here. And when I think about it, it can seem strange beyond telling that this particular bunch of us should be here on this little patch of ground in this little patch of time, and I think of the other times and places I might have lived, the other kinds of man I might have been. But there is something else. There are moments when the heart is generous, and then it knows that for better or worse our lives are woven together here, one with another and with the place and all the living things.” [p. 210]<br />•	From Nathan Coulter to Hannah when she makes a remark about her children: “…I said, “I just wanted them to have a better chance than I had.” Nathan said, “Don’t complain about the chance you had,” in the same exact way he used to tell the boys, “Don’t cuss the weather.” Sometimes you can say dreadful things without knowing it. Nathan understood this better than I did. Like several of his one-sentence conversations, this one stuck in my mind and finally changed it. The change came too late, maybe, but it turned my mind inside out like a sock. … Was I sorry that I had known my parents and Grandmam and Ora Finley and the Catletts and the Feltners, and that I had married Virgil and come to live in Port William, and that I had lived on after Virgil’s death to marry Nathan and come to our place to raise our family and live among the Coulters and the rest of our family? Well, that was the chance I had. …The chance you had is the life you’ve got. You can make complaints about what people, including you, make of their lives after they have got them, and about what people make of other people’s lives, even about your children being gone, but you mustn’t wish for another life. You mustn’t want to be somebody else. <br />Berry tells us the life story of Jayber Crow, from young boy to old man. He is the barber of Port William, as well as its gravedigger and church bellringer and janitor. He is not known for being loquacious but is quite observant of what goes on around him, and he lets us know what is going around him, and what happens to various people of the membership of Port William.<br /><br />What did I like about this book?<br />•	Some of the characters and their decentness. I liked Burley Coulter because he was good-hearted, and he helped out Jayber at just the right times. And I liked Athey Keith, a farmer who preferred the old-fashioned way of doing things because they were tried-and-true rather than jumping onto the newest invention that might or might not be as good, in the long run, as the old way of doing things. As well as his basic decentness. <br />•	And of course I liked a good deal of the writing.<br /><br />What didn’t I like? I don’t like to perseverate on this because I look at Wendell Berry and his wide-ranging literary prowess in fiction, poetry, non-fiction, the awards he has garnered, and some of the stands he has taken on different issues including the environment (he puts his money where his mouth is) and sustainable farming, among other things. That’s a lot!!! So I will keep it short…<br />•	Some of the book, to me, was less the musings of Jayber Crow and what was in his head, but more on Wendell Berry — for example, how he felt about Christianity (what might be taught in a Protestant church on a Sunday versus what he saw Jesus Christ and his teachings as). It seemed that Jayber was a convenient entity to be the mouthpiece of Berry and what he wanted to tell the reader. <br />•	Jayber was married to Mattie Keith but she was not married to Jayber Crow. Huh? That seeming discrepancy is due to the fact that he had taken Mattie as his wife in his head, not in reality. And he lived a good deal of his life married to her, although she was wed to another man (who he heartily disliked). That was just too bizarre for me. Not only that, the night he made that decision to wed Mattie, he rather unceremoniously ran out on a woman who he was seeing (in fairness to him, they had no plans of getting married).<br />•	The length of the book, 363 pages. <br /><br />Reviews:<br />•	<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://movies2.nytimes.com/books/00/10/01/bib/001001.rv135617.html">https://movies2.nytimes.com/books/00/...</a> <br />•	blog site, <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://hopeisthewordblog.com/2008/08/09/book-review-jayber-crow-by-wendell-berry/">https://hopeisthewordblog.com/2008/08...</a> <br />•	<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://thecresset.org/2011/Michaelmas/Eads_M2011.html">http://thecresset.org/2011/Michaelmas...</a> <br />•	<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.rambles.net/berry_jayber.html">https://www.rambles.net/berry_jayber....</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="7680490947762452523" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_214065530" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/214065530" />
    <a title="Melki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5293536-melki"><img alt="Melki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308071306p2/5293536.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/214065530?book_show_action=true">Nov 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melki" href="/user/show/5293536-melki">Melki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=uptown-book-coven">uptown-book-coven</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer214065530" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9699994339670559078"><b>
  <i>I'd had the idea, once, that if I could get the chance before I died I would read all the good books there were. Now I began to see that I wasn't apt to make it. This disappointed me, for I really wanted to read them all.</i>
</b><br/><br/>It says a lot that I started this book on September 23rd, and didn't finish it until November 12th. I meant to read a chapter each day, I really did, but I didn't exactly wake up each morning saying, gee . . . I wonder what madcap adventure ole Jayber's up to today?<br/><br/>'Cause he</span>
  <span id="freeText9699994339670559078" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>I'd had the idea, once, that if I could get the chance before I died I would read all the good books there were. Now I began to see that I wasn't apt to make it. This disappointed me, for I really wanted to read them all.</i>
</b><br /><br />It says a lot that I started this book on September 23rd, and didn't finish it until November 12th. I meant to read a chapter each day, I really did, but I didn't exactly wake up each morning saying, gee . . . I wonder what madcap adventure ole Jayber's up to today?<br /><br />'Cause he really didn't get up to much. He stood in place in his little barber shop with no running water, cut other men's hair, and watched the world go by. And, it went by without him. <br /><br />The big problem is Jayber himself. Crow seems to be a minor character in his own life; an audience member, rather than an actor. Sometimes, this can work, IF a blank slate of a character meets and interacts with lots of quirky characters. But, there is no quirk factor in this book. Jayber's friends and neighbors are as bland and uninteresting as he is.<br /> <br />There is no doubt this is a beautifully written book, but there were many times I felt like quitting. In the end, I'm glad I didn't. Part III was wonderful, though sad, and melancholic, as Jayber witnesses the inevitable decline of his beloved small town.<br /> <br /><b>
  <i>It had about come to the point where nobody wanted to live in Port William who wasn't already in the habit of living there, or who could afford to live anyplace else.</i>
</b><br /><br />Most people seem to have liked this one way more than I did, so I'm chalking it up to both wrong book at the wrong time, and a little bit of too many other titles on the stack calling my name. <br /><br />Berry sure can write some pretty words, though.<br /> <br /> <b>
  <i>I am a man who has hoped, in time, that his life, when poured out at the end, would say, "Good-good-good-good-good!" like a gallon jug of the prime local spirit. I am a man of losses, regrets, and griefs. I am a man full of love. I am a man of faith.</i>
</b></span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3271238922" />
    <a title="Pedro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/73200741-pedro"><img alt="Pedro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599994103p2/73200741.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3271238922?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pedro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pedro" href="/user/show/73200741-pedro">Pedro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3271238922" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10110569285611924235">Reading this novel was like going through 365 pages of meditation practice. <br/><br/>I’d come from work, read a few pages (chapters aren’t long) and literally get lost in the life of Jayber Crow. His was what you’d call a normal life, but then, I believe all life stories can be as compelling and believable as this one if they’re told in the right way!<br/><br/>This is not a novel of “fireworks” and overly dramatic plot twists which (probably) means that lovers of fast paced stories will certainly get frustrated ab</span>
  <span id="freeText10110569285611924235" style="display:none">Reading this novel was like going through 365 pages of meditation practice. <br /><br />I’d come from work, read a few pages (chapters aren’t long) and literally get lost in the life of Jayber Crow. His was what you’d call a normal life, but then, I believe all life stories can be as compelling and believable as this one if they’re told in the right way!<br /><br />This is not a novel of “fireworks” and overly dramatic plot twists which (probably) means that lovers of fast paced stories will certainly get frustrated about how slow the story unfolds. <br /><br />Jayber lived in a different time; a time when people didn’t have 1000 profiles on different social media websites, a time when people didn’t spend most of their daily lives watching TV and checking their mobile phones.<br /><br />Can you remember a time where people actually interacted, talked and laughed together? Can you? A time when people payed attention to the changing seasons, the cycle of life and the way they’d fit into that cycle? Do you miss those times? If so this is the perfect novel for you. <br /><br />And if you (really) like good writing then you can’t go wrong with this one. <br /><br />Definitely check this one out if you love authors like Niall Williams and/or Wallace Stegner. <br /><br />I believe life is like a river.<br />And we all know where we are running to.<br /></span>

<div id="review_644491070" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/644491070" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/644491070?book_show_action=true">Jun 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=philo-psychol">philo-psychol</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible">audible</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=relationships">relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=southern">southern</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=alt">alt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=hf">hf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=religion">religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer644491070" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14239618292801017533">What do I think of this book? I absolutely hated parts and other parts totally blew me over, the words were so perfect. The author IS an acclaimed poet. I was never indifferent to this book. Either I was furious or astounded by the quality of the writing. Should I give it one star for all the times I felt like dumping it immediately? I cannot give it two or three stars because they are lukewarm ratings. I was never lukewarm to this book. Yes, I liked it a lot, four stars it is. I will explain wh</span>
  <span id="freeText14239618292801017533" style="display:none">What do I think of this book? I absolutely hated parts and other parts totally blew me over, the words were so perfect. The author IS an acclaimed poet. I was never indifferent to this book. Either I was furious or astounded by the quality of the writing. Should I give it one star for all the times I felt like dumping it immediately? I cannot give it two or three stars because they are lukewarm ratings. I was never lukewarm to this book. Yes, I liked it a lot, four stars it is. I will explain what I liked and what I absolutely hated.<br /><br />When Wendell Berry describes nature - a river, a forest, a foggy morning – it is not just beautiful, it is completely accurate. A river is something you hear and see. You feel its presence, and all this is conveyed in his words. Me, I adore walking in the woods or along a beach so I felt very attached to Berry’s words. <br /><br />Humor. There is lots of humor. Tongue in cheek humor and that is my favorite. Great dialogs too.<br /><br />I look at the story as a whole and I feel the message the author wants to convey is perfectly demonstrated by the events, by what happens, particularly its ending. This is a book about a barber (Jayber Crow) in Port William, Kentucky. He tells us about his life living through the events of the 1900s. He speaks of not only his life but all the people of the town, since being the barber he comes to know everyone. This is not a book of historical fiction; you do not read this to learn about either of the wars or the Vietnam War or the Depression, all of which he lives through. He never went to war since his heart disqualified him. He was orphaned twice, but I will not explain that. Read the book instead. He was first educated to become a priest, but he realized it wasn’t his calling. He did have faith. He philosophizes and thinks and questions. All of the things he lived through shape his personality. Berry creates a character that is believable. <br /><br />The author has a pet peeve and he speaks through Jayber. Agriculture has become big business and this is just not good in the long run! Natural resources are being wasted. Small town life, based on sharing and trade where everyone knows each other is always better than big business. Small scale is always better than large scale production. But it is here that I got so annoyed with the book. I agree with the author’s/barber’s point of view. I am not opposed to the message, but it is repeated and said over and over and over to the point where I just wanted him to zip his mouth. Enough! I get it. I agree. I am not an idiot. I don’t need a lecture. Will you shut up! Do you understand how annoyed I got?!<br /><br />There. If you can stand a little too much philosophizing and preaching and religious talk, which I could not quite swallow, you will also be given a good story that holds together, where the characters feel real, with lines that will make you smile or laugh or chuckle and most everyone will agree with the message imparted. The author is a poet ……except in those parts when he is proselytizing through Jayber. <br /><br />P.S. Paul Michael narrated the audiobook I listened to. I liked his southern dialect. I liked the speed, which is rather slow, but I did want him to hurry up when Jayber went on and on and on with his proselytizing soliloquies. The women all sounded the same, and that annoyed me because their personalities were different!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14239618292801017533" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <span id="freeText5741672149176571287" style="display:none">Jayber Crow is an orphan twice over who ends up in an orphanage at age ten. <div id="review_1397606764" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1397606764" />
    <a title="Laysee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4863523-laysee"><img alt="Laysee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459741824p2/4863523.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1397606764?book_show_action=true">Sep 21, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laysee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laysee" href="/user/show/4863523-laysee">Laysee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Laysee by: </span>
      Steve Sckenda
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4863523-laysee?shelf=five-star-books">five-star-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4863523-laysee?shelf=lifetime-favorites">lifetime-favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1397606764" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14165596604663653258"><br/>Jayber Crow is a beautifully written novel that has heart, soul, and spirit. I appreciated it most for the still center that was bound up in its narrator, Jayber Crow, a man given to solitude. The novel had a quiet appeal like the river that flowed through the fictional town of Port William in Kentucky where the story was set. It traced the life story of Jayber Crow and his relationship with the people in Port William. It was easy to be drawn into young Jayber’s storm-tossed life but it soon bec</span>
  <span id="freeText14165596604663653258" style="display:none"><br />Jayber Crow is a beautifully written novel that has heart, soul, and spirit. I appreciated it most for the still center that was bound up in its narrator, Jayber Crow, a man given to solitude. The novel had a quiet appeal like the river that flowed through the fictional town of Port William in Kentucky where the story was set. It traced the life story of Jayber Crow and his relationship with the people in Port William. It was easy to be drawn into young Jayber’s storm-tossed life but it soon became obvious that Wendell Berry had deep thoughts to share about freedom, independence, love, faith, faithfulness, loneliness, grace and forgiveness. The novel also celebrated the beauty, comfort and solace that abounded in nature and it offered many opportunities for lovely detours into the woods. When a book serves up a protagonist I can respect, food for thought, the call of the wild, it is immensely satisfying to me.<br /><br />Jayber Crow was orphaned at age 4, briefly adopted by a loving paternal grand aunt (Aunt Cordie) and her husband (Uncle Othy) until their death, before he ended up in The Good Shepherd Orphanage at age 10. The chapter on The Good Shepherd was gently heartbreaking. I felt for the young Jayber feeling homesick, lonely and displaced. But I was also impressed by his ability to find pleasure and strength in little touches of beauty that surrounded him or to find congenial company in a good book in the orphanage library. Like in other novels (Anthony Doerr’s “All The Light We Could Not See”, Jeanette Winterson’s “Why Be Happy When You Can Be Normal”), the library opened up a world of knowledge and the power of words for the powerless individual. The adult Jayber signed up for literature courses at the university. His love for reading endeared him to me as did his love for solitude and quietness.<br /><br />It is interesting to contemplate Jayber’s calling or vocation. Fresh out of high school, he felt he was called to be a preacher. However, he struggled with theological questions for which he had no answers. Old Grit, his professor, told him "You have been given questions to which you cannot be given answers. You will have to live them out - perhaps a little at a time." And Jayber did and in a way that caught him by surprise. At age 22, Jayber became the barber of Port William. The barbershop was a gathering place for the men of the town. Behind the barber's chair, Jayber had access to privileged communication, in his case as an unofficial counselor of sorts. "Talk..draws to a barber as water draws to low ground." He became an astute observer of life and despite his aloofness and preference to be a bystander, Jayber became more closely knit to the town folks than he ever thought possible or wanted to. His friendship with a small circle of men (such as Athey Keith, Burley Coulter) was precious. <br /><br />Jayber struck me as a young man with an ancient soul. He had a leaning toward aloneness, owing perhaps to his early life experiences. He was constantly given to unrelenting feelings of loss and memories of old times past. His grief had a complexity that was peculiar and hard to fathom: "This grief had something in it of generosity, some nearness to joy. In a strange way it added to me what I had lost." In the same way, Jayber had a strange, unspoken love for that one special woman (Mattie Chatham) that I found perplexing. He was a man inclined toward inaction. The Christmas party at which he realized he was in love with Mattie read almost like a conversion experience of the “I felt my heart strangely warmed” variant. But his response was typical of his normal responses to everything else: "I had been utterly changed, and had not stirred." "There was nothing to do but submit to the trial of it." The submission took the form of a quiet vow of marriage to Mattie who never had any inkling of his feelings for her. In later years, the unplanned times he and Mattie met by the river to which they had separately gone to seek comfort and solace were, he thought, “a hope seen from afar, that must be with patience waited for.” The Bible has a word for this – faith. How does a person love so deeply and faithfully when he loves so hopelessly? It is sad but beautiful.<br /><br />My favorite part of this novel is Part III, which expressed issues surrounding love that were powerful and profound. They begged re-reading, which was what I did after the last page was turned. One of the loveliest chapters in Part III described Jayber's new life in a new house by the river. I could picture it and wish I lived there. It was written in an unpretentious manner that elicited gladness and a desire to linger a little longer over the repose nature afforded. <br /><br />I believe Jayber Crow is not a book for everyone. The events unfolded in Jayber Crow’s realm of thought and contemplation. It is a book to be read slowly perhaps by the banks of a calming river.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14165596604663653258" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2842898811" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2842898811" />
    <a title="Joy D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50297885-joy-d"><img alt="Joy D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1450639623p2/50297885.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2842898811?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joy D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joy D" href="/user/show/50297885-joy-d">Joy D</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/9284904-jayber-crow">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=north-america">north-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=social-commentary">social-commentary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=american-south">american-south</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=spirituality">spirituality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=zck">zck</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=friendship">friendship</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=environmental">environmental</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2842898811" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5027839897383487728">Life story of Jonah Crow, nicknamed Jayber, barber of the small farming community of Port William, Kentucky, from his birth in 1914 to his life in retirement. He is orphaned at an early age, briefly attends divinity school, and eventually makes his home above his barbershop. He remains a bachelor but cherishes a woman from afar. The narrative follows the intersecting lives of Jayber and the various residents of Port William. <br/><br/>This is a classic celebration of the pastoral life. It is critical of i</span>
  <span id="freeText5027839897383487728" style="display:none">Life story of Jonah Crow, nicknamed Jayber, barber of the small farming community of Port William, Kentucky, from his birth in 1914 to his life in retirement. He is orphaned at an early age, briefly attends divinity school, and eventually makes his home above his barbershop. He remains a bachelor but cherishes a woman from afar. The narrative follows the intersecting lives of Jayber and the various residents of Port William. <br /><br />This is a classic celebration of the pastoral life. It is critical of industrialization. Farmer Athey Keith uses traditional farming methods – plowing with mules, rotating crops, and saving funds for emergencies. His son-in-law, Troy Chatham, represents the modern approach to “agribusiness,” borrowing heavily, buying machines, and depleting the land. <br /><br />Jayber tells his own story, so we are privy to his thoughts. He questions religion and theology, leading to his departure from seminary school, but values faithful devotion and always cares about those around him, even to the point of embracing people he dislikes (not always successfully). The concept of heaven is also explored. <br /><br />The characters are richly developed. By the end, I felt like they were my neighbors. Jayber’s voice is particularly strong. It felt like an older relative telling me his nostalgic stories. Berry’s writing is lyrical. There are passages that made me want to soak them in and ponder them for a while. The story is chronological, and proceeds at a leisurely pace. Most of the plot describes small-town life, farming, and community connections. <br /><br /><i>“Maybe you can imagine it: the moon hanging all alone out in the sky, its light pouring down over everything and filling the valley, and under the moonlight the woods, making a darkness, and within the darkness a little room of firelight, and within the firelight several men talking, some standing, some sitting on stools of piled rocks or on logs, some sitting or squatting or kneeling around a spot swept clear of leaves where they were playing cards, and all around you could hear the whippoorwills. Nearly everybody there had a coal oil lantern, most of them unlit to save oil. One of the two or three that were lighted hung from a low limb to illuminate the card game.”</i><br /><br />Themes include belonging, independence, dealing with change, and the joys of living a simple life. Berry advocates stewardship of the earth and compassion for its inhabitants. I enjoyed spending time in Port William and found this book delightful. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5027839897383487728" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_2636003400" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2636003400" />
    <a title="Ellie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2908629-ellie"><img alt="Ellie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1399799985p2/2908629.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2636003400?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ellie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ellie" href="/user/show/2908629-ellie">Ellie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=2019gpchallenge">2019gpchallenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=2019indchal">2019indchal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2636003400" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16042380483612391744">This may be one of my all-time favorite books. It reminds me of another beautiful book that I loved, Stoner, but unlike Stoner (which left me depressed and sad), Jayber Crow lifted me up. A wonderful line that to me sums up the book says, "There is a light that includes the darkness." And although Jayber Crow contains many sad things, there is a light that shines through it and keeps it from overcoming the light (to paraphrase the gospel John).<br/><br/>Jayber Crow lives, by many standards, a difficult li</span>
  <span id="freeText16042380483612391744" style="display:none">This may be one of my all-time favorite books. It reminds me of another beautiful book that I loved, Stoner, but unlike Stoner (which left me depressed and sad), Jayber Crow lifted me up. A wonderful line that to me sums up the book says, "There is a light that includes the darkness." And although Jayber Crow contains many sad things, there is a light that shines through it and keeps it from overcoming the light (to paraphrase the gospel John).<br /><br />Jayber Crow lives, by many standards, a difficult life. He is orphaned twice by the age of 10, raised in an orphanage, believed himself to be called to be a minister but decides he is not meant for that life when questions come to him that seem (to him) to make him unfit for the ministry. However, in the course of this book, Jayber comes to be an intensely spiritual man who finds God in the world around him as well as in the people (good and bad) that live in the small town of Port William, a town Berry has placed several of his stories in.<br /><br />Jaybe was born in (or near) Port William and finds himself drawn back to it after leaving school. He has drifted into the work of a barber and that's what he spends the rest of his working life in Port William doing. As barber, he is a spectator in the life of the town, both a part of it and apart from it, watching. As time goes by, Jayber's heart seems to grow larger or his spirituality does and he draws from this small town--almost a village--lessons about human life. <br /><br />The world effects Port William--World War II, the Korean War, Vietnam claim their victims and what Jayber calls "The Great Economy" changes its ways of working and living forever (and not for the better) but Port William does not effect the world. But in Port William, Berry shows us an entire world but one in which there is room for every individual, people are not swallowed up by size or multitudes but remain intact, the good with the bad within each one and the bonds that connect them all.<br /><br />Jaybe never marries but he has a secret love that guides his life. While this made me sad, it also moved me in other ways. Love is not depressing, not when it illuminates a life, whether or not it is requited. Or so it seemed to me in this book.<br /><br />Jayber Crow has line after beautiful line of sentences that are almost (or actually) poetry. Berry's skill as a poet focuses this work and creates a luminescent prose that almost shimmers. There's hardly a line I wouldn't underline and many that I reread just to enjoy their rhythms and images again. Some made me cry just by how exquisite they are.<br /><br />This is a book that satisfies on so many levels. It's slow moving but that makes room for the reader to enter into the world Berry has created and live with the people who feel like more than mere characters in a book. I'm a city woman with little desire for a rural life but this world made me recognize a different kind of life that is valuable in its own way. Unfortunately, one of the things the book shows is how that life is disappearing or has already disappeared.<br /><br />This book is an elegy and a tribute to a kind of living and being together but it is also a testimony to how we still can live. It is a vision of how we are all connected with each other whether or not we like the other person or even know we are connected. At the end of the story, Jayber accidentally tears a spider's web and tries (unsuccessfully) to repair it. His town is like that web and is being destroyed by the carelessness of people who will never know what they have destroyed.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16042380483612391744" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3043547507?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Negin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Negin" href="/user/show/6326169-negin">Negin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/9284904-jayber-crow">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6326169-negin?shelf=usa">usa</a>
          </div>
